../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-        Therefore, a host will reasonably be able to trust a Redirect
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-        that comes from a router.
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-        However, it will NOT be reasonable for a host to trust another
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-        host.  Suppose that the target host in the examples of Section
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt:        4.1 is untrustworthy; there is no way to prevent its issuing a
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-        new Redirect to some other destination, anywhere in the
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-        Internet.  On the other hand, this exposure is no worse than it
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-        was; the target host, once subverted, could always act as a
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-        hidden router to forward traffic elsewhere.
../data/rfc/rfc1620.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   *Trust:*
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-      Data authenticity is distinct from data trustworthiness, though
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-      the two concepts are related.  A packet is authentic if it has a
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt:      valid name-to-content binding.  A packet is trustworthy, i.e., it
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-      comes from a reputable or trusted origin, if this binding is valid
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-      in the context of a trust model.  The trust model provides
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-      assurance that the name used for a given piece of content is
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-      appropriate for the value of the content.  Section 6 discusses
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-      this further.

../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   that utilize the terms most certainly do.  Readers should look at
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   those specifications (e.g., [RFC8569] and [NDN]) where various
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   security considerations are addressed in detail.
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   Some of the terms in this document use the words "trust",
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt:   "trustworthy", or "trust model".  We intend that these have their
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   colloquial meanings; however, much work on trust, and specifically on
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   trust schemas for ICN architectures, has been published in the last
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   few years.  For example, it is useful to look at [SCHEMATIZING-TRUST]
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   for more information on the approaches taken to formalize notions of
../data/rfc/rfc8793.txt-   trust for current NDN and CCNx systems.

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-1.  Introduction
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Several public and commercial services need location information to
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   possible, it is important to be able to determine the source of the
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   call in a post-incident investigation, so as to be able to enforce
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   accountability.
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   This document defines terminology (including the meaning of
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:   "trustworthy location") in Section 1.1, reviews existing work in
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Section 1.2, describes threat models in Section 2, outlines potential
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   mitigation techniques in Section 3, covers trust assessment in
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Section 4, and discusses security considerations in Section 5.
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-1.1.  Terminology
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-Tschofenig, et al.            Informational                     [Page 3]
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:RFC 7378                  Trustworthy Location             December 2014
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   We use the definitions of "Internet Access Provider (IAP)", "Internet
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Service Provider (ISP)", and "Voice Service Provider (VSP)" found in
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   "Requirements for Emergency Context Resolution with Internet
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-Tschofenig, et al.            Informational                     [Page 4]
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:RFC 7378                  Trustworthy Location             December 2014
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Additionally, the terms "location-by-value" (LbyV), "location-by-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   reference" (LbyR), "Location Configuration Protocol", "Location
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Dereference Protocol", and "Location Uniform Resource Identifier"
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   (URI) are reused from "Requirements for a Location-by-Reference
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Mechanism" [RFC5808].
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:   "Trustworthy Location" is defined as location information that can be
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   attributed to a trusted source, has been protected against
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:   modification in transmit, and has been assessed as trustworthy.
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   "Location Trust Assessment" refers to the process by which the
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   reliability of location information can be assessed.  This topic is
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   discussed in Section 4.
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-Tschofenig, et al.            Informational                     [Page 5]
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:RFC 7378                  Trustworthy Location             December 2014
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-1.2.1.  Location
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   The Internet architecture for emergency calling is described in
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-Tschofenig, et al.            Informational                     [Page 6]
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:RFC 7378                  Trustworthy Location             December 2014
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Although the infrastructure for location-based routing described in
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   [RFC6443] was developed for use in emergency services, [RFC6442]
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   supports conveyance of location within non-emergency calls as well as
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-Tschofenig, et al.            Informational                     [Page 7]
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:RFC 7378                  Trustworthy Location             December 2014
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   A particularly dangerous form of hoax call is "swatting" -- a hoax
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   emergency call that draws a response from law enforcement prepared
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   for a violent confrontation (e.g., a fake hostage situation that
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   so-called non-service initialized (NSI) handsets, or where ownership
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   of the SIM card cannot be determined, the frequency of hoax calls has
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   often been unacceptably high [TASMANIA] [UK] [SA].
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   However, there are few documented cases of hoax calls that have
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:   arisen from conveyance of untrustworthy location information within
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   an emergency call, which is the focus of this document.
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   This requires that a standardized VoIP profile be deployed at every

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   the Location Recipient has additional information on which to base
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   their trust assessment, such as the validity of the signature, the

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Real-time validation of the timestamp contained within PIDF-LO
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   objects (reflecting the time at which the location was determined) is
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   also challenging.  To address time-shifting attacks, the "timestamp"
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   element of the PIDF-LO, defined in [RFC3863], can be examined and
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   compared against timestamps included within the enclosing SIP
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   message, to determine whether the location data is sufficiently
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   fresh.  However, the timestamp only represents an assertion by the
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt:   LIS, which may or may not be trustworthy.  For example, the Recipient
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   of the signed PIDF-LO may not know whether the LIS supports time
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   synchronization, or whether it is possible to reset the LIS clock
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   manually without detection.  Even if the timestamp was valid at the
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   time location was determined, a time period may elapse between when
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   the PIDF-LO was provided and when it is conveyed to the Recipient.

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-5.  Security Considerations
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   Although it is important to ensure that location information cannot
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   While manually mounting the attacks described in Section 2 is
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   non-trivial, the attacks described in this document can be automated.
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   While manually carrying out a location theft would require that the
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   The intent of the location privacy architecture was to provide strong
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-   privacy protections, as noted in [RFC6280], Section 1.1:
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
--
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-      sees the passing of the SIP message as part of the delivery to
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-      authorized recipients, rather than as retransmission.  SIP
../data/rfc/rfc7378.txt-      entities are still enjoined from passing these messages

../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-3.2.2 Usage of ESP
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   ESP works between hosts, between a host and a security gateway, or
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   between security gateways. This support for security gateways permits
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt:   trustworthy networks behind a security gateway to omit encryption and
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   thereby avoid the performance and monetary costs of encryption, while
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt:   still providing confidentiality for traffic transiting untrustworthy
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   network segments.  When both hosts directly implement ESP and there
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   is no intervening security gateway, then they may use the Transport-
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   mode (where only the upper layer protocol data (e.g., TCP or UDP) is
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   encrypted and there is no encrypted IP header).  This mode reduces
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   both the bandwidth consumed and the protocol processing costs for
--
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   confidentiality is not considered necessary within the particular
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   operational environment, the Authentication Header is used to provide
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   authentication for the entire packet, including cryptographic binding
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   of the sensitivity level to the IP header and user data.  This is a
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   significant improvement over labeled IPv4 networks where the label is
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt:   trusted even though it is not trustworthy because there is no
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   authentication or cryptographic binding of the label to the IP header
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   and user data.  IPv6 will normally use implicit sensitivity labels
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   that are part of the Security Association but not transmitted with
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   each packet instead of using explicit sensitivity labels.  All
../data/rfc/rfc1825.txt-   explicit IP sensitivity labels MUST be authenticated using either

../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-2.4.  Betrayal By Trusted Server
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   Another variation on the packet interception attack is the trusted
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt:   server that turns out not to be so trustworthy, whether by accident
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   or by intent.  Many client machines are only configured with stub
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   resolvers, and use trusted servers to perform all of their DNS
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   queries on their behalf.  In many cases the trusted server is
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   furnished by the user's ISP and advertised to the client via DHCP or
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   PPP options.  Besides accidental betrayal of this trust relationship
--
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   to promote some other goal such as furthering a business partnership
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   between the ISP and some third party.
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   This problem is particularly acute for frequent travelers who carry
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   their own equipment and expect it to work in much the same way
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt:   wherever they go.  Such travelers need trustworthy DNS service
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   without regard to who operates the network into which their equipment
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   is currently plugged or what brand of middle boxes the local
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   infrastructure might use.
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   While the obvious solution to this problem would be for the client to
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt:   choose a more trustworthy server, in practice this may not be an
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   option for the client.  In many network environments a client machine
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   has only a limited set of recursive name servers from which to
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt:   choose, and none of them may be particularly trustworthy.  In extreme
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   cases, port filtering or other forms of packet interception may
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   prevent the client host from being able to run an iterative resolver
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   even if the owner of the client machine is willing and able to do so.
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   Thus, while the initial source of this problem is not a DNS protocol
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   attack per se, this sort of betrayal is a threat to DNS clients, and
--
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   attacker.  The defense against this is the same as with a packet
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   interception attack: the resolver must either check DNSSEC signatures
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   itself or use TSIG (or equivalent) to authenticate the server that it
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   has chosen to trust.  Note that use of TSIG does not by itself
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt:   guarantee that a name server is at all trustworthy: all TSIG can do
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   is help a resolver protect its communication with a name server that
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   it has already decided to trust for other reasons.  Protecting a
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   resolver's communication with a server that's giving out bogus
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-   answers is not particularly useful.
../data/rfc/rfc3833.txt-
--
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   sensitive information within inter-process tokens, or to
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   cryptographically protect them, many implementations will be unable
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   to avoid placing key material or other sensitive data within them.
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   It is the application's responsibility to ensure that inter-process
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   tokens are protected in transit, and transferred only to processes
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt:   that are trustworthy.  An inter-process token is represented using a
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   byte array emitted from the export method of the GSSContext
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   interface.  The receiver of the inter-process token would initialize
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   an GSSContext object with this token to create a new context.  Once a
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   context has been exported, the GSSContext object is invalidated and
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   is no longer available.
--
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   to either avoid placing such sensitive information within inter-
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   process tokens or to encrypt the token before returning it to the
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   application, in a typical GSS-API implementation, this may not be
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   possible.  Thus, the application must take care to protect the
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt-   inter-process token, and ensure that any process to which the token
../data/rfc/rfc5653.txt:   is transferred is trustworthy.

../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-   A corrupted MA could report falsified information to the Collector.
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-   Whether this can be effectively mitigated depends on the platform on
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-   which the MA is deployed.  However, where the MA is deployed on a
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-   customer-controlled device, then the reported data is to some degree
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt:   inherently untrustworthy.  Further, a sophisticated party could
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-   distort some Measurement Methods, perhaps by dropping or delaying
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-   packets for example.  This suggests that the network operator should
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-   be cautious about relying on Measurement Results for action such as
../data/rfc/rfc7594.txt-   refunding fees if a service level agreement is not met.

../data/rfc/rfc5585.txt-   has not been modified between the time of signing and the time of
../data/rfc/rfc5585.txt-   verifying.  If it has been changed in any way, then the message will
../data/rfc/rfc5585.txt-   not be verified successfully with DKIM.
../data/rfc/rfc5585.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc5585.txt:   of trust associated with the message.  If it was an untrustworthy
../data/rfc/rfc5585.txt-   message when initially sent, the verifier can be certain that the
../data/rfc/rfc5585.txt:   message will be equally untrustworthy upon receipt and successful
../data/rfc/rfc5585.txt-   verification.

../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      1. (I) Information system usage: The extent to which someone who
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      relies on a system can have confidence that the system meets its
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      specifications, i.e., that the system does what it claims to do
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      and does not perform unwanted functions. (See: trust level.)
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt:      (C) "trusted vs. trustworthy": In discussing a system or system
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      process or object, this Glossary (and industry usage) prefers the
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      term "trusted" to describe a system that operates as expected,
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      according to design and policy. When the trust can also be
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      guaranteed in some convincing way, such as through formal analysis
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt:      or code review, the system is termed "trustworthy"; this differs
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt:      from the ABA Guidelines definition (see: trustworthy system).
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      2. (I) PKI usage: A relationship between a certificate user and a
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      CA in which the user acts according to the assumption that the CA
../data/rfc/rfc2828.txt-      creates only valid digital certificates.

../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-3.1.  Corporate Intranet Model
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-   In a corporate intranet or other network where all nodes are under
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-   one administrative domain, the nodes may be considered to be reliable
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-   at the IP layer.  Thus, once a node has been accepted to be a member
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt:   of the network, it is assumed to behave in a trustworthy manner.
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-   Under this model, if the network is physically secured or if the link
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-   layer is cryptographically secured to the extent needed, no other
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-   protection is needed for IPv6 ND, as long as none of the nodes become
../data/rfc/rfc3756.txt-   compromised.  For example, a wired LAN with 802.1x access control or
--

../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   In scenarios with intermediary nodes such as proxies or gateways,
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   transport layer security such as (D)TLS only protects data hop-by-
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   hop.  As a consequence, the intermediary nodes can read and modify
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   any information.  The trust model where all intermediary nodes are
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt:   considered trustworthy is problematic, not only from a privacy
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   perspective, but also from a security perspective, as the
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   intermediaries are free to delete resources on sensors and falsify
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   commands to actuators (such as "unlock door", "start fire alarm",
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   "raise bridge").  Even in the rare cases where all the owners of the
../data/rfc/rfc8613.txt-   intermediary nodes are fully trusted, attacks and data breaches make

../data/rfc/rfc6698.txt-   is no problem distributing the signed keys.  Most significantly, the
../data/rfc/rfc6698.txt-   keys associated with a domain name can only be signed by a key
../data/rfc/rfc6698.txt-   associated with the parent of that domain name; for example, the keys

../data/rfc/rfc5765.txt-   trust and recommendation where each new applicant has to be known and

../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   Evidence Record is closely related to Time-Stamping techniques.
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   However, Time-Stamps as defined in [RFC3161] can cover only a single
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   unit of data and do not provide processing rules for maintaining a
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   long-term stability of Time-Stamps applied over a data object.
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   Evidence for an archive object is created by acquiring a Time-Stamp
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt:   from a trustworthy authority for a specific value that is
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   unambiguously related to a single or more data objects.  Relationship
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   between several data objects and a single Time-Stamped value is
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   addressed using a hash tree, a technique first described by Merkle
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   [MER1980] and later in [RFC4998], with data structures and procedures
../data/rfc/rfc6283.txt-   as specified in this document.  The Evidence Record Syntax enables

../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   Phishing
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   Phishing is a form of criminal activity using social engineering
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   techniques.  It is characterized by attempts to fraudulently acquire
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   sensitive information, such as passwords and credit card details, by
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt:   masquerading as a trustworthy person or business in an apparently
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   official electronic communication.  Phishing is typically carried out
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   using spoofed websites, email, or an instant message.  The term
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   phishing derives from password harvesting and the use of increasingly
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   sophisticated lures to "fish" for users' financial information and
../data/rfc/rfc4948.txt-   passwords.
--

../data/rfc/rfc5752.txt-   to produce the SignerInfo apply.
../data/rfc/rfc5752.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc5752.txt-   entities, the entity creating the signature must ensure that the hash
../data/rfc/rfc5752.txt:   comes from a "trustworthy" source.  This can be partially mitigated
../data/rfc/rfc5752.txt-   by requiring that multiple hashes using different algorithms are
../data/rfc/rfc5752.txt-   provided.
../data/rfc/rfc5752.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc5752.txt-   algorithms used in the signer attribute are 'cracked'.  It is not

../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-   When a link enters the Anomalous state with respect to a parameter,
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-   this is an indication that LSPs using that link might also no longer
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-   be in compliance with their performance bounds.  It can also be
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-   considered an indication that something is changing that link and so
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt:   it might no longer be trustworthy to carry performance-critical
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-   traffic.  Naturally, which performance criteria are important for a
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-   particular LSP is dependent upon the LSP's configuration; thus, the
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-   compliance of a link with respect to a particular link performance
../data/rfc/rfc7823.txt-   objective is indicated per performance criterion.

../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-7.2.  Misleading Results
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-   Until some form of service for querying the reputation of a sending
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-   agent is widely deployed, the existence of this header field
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt:   indicating a "pass" does not render the message trustworthy.  It is
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-   possible for an arriving piece of spam or other undesirable mail to
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-   pass checks by several of the methods enumerated above (e.g., a piece
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-   of spam signed using [DKIM] by the originator of the spam, which
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-   might be a spammer or a compromised system).  In particular, this
../data/rfc/rfc7601.txt-   issue is not resolved by forged header field removal discussed above.

../data/rfc/rfc6819.txt-   manage the power associated with an access token.  A scope can be
../data/rfc/rfc6819.txt-   controlled by the authorization server and/or the end user in order

../data/rfc/rfc6819.txt-   this token is sent over non-secure channels.  A scope is typically
../data/rfc/rfc6819.txt-   complemented by a restriction on a token's lifetime.

../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   Identified Mail [DKIM] protocol permits associating a validated
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   identifier to a message.  This association is cryptographically
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   strong, and is an improvement over the prior state of affairs, but it
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   does not distinguish between identifiers of good actors and bad.
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   Even when it is possible to validate the domain name in an author
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt:   field (e.g., "trustworthy.example.com" in
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt:   "john.doe@trustworthy.example.com"), there is no basis for knowing
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   whether it is associated with a good actor who is worthy of trust.
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   As a practical matter, both bad actors and good adopt basic
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   authentication mechanisms like DKIM.  In fact, bad actors tend to
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   adopt them even more rapidly than the good actors do in the hope that
../data/rfc/rfc7070.txt-   some receivers will confuse identity authentication with identity

../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   A weaker form of identity assertion is possible using the P-Asserted-
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   Identity header field [5], but this technique requires mutual trust
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   among all domains.  Unfortunately, this becomes exponentially harder
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   to provide as the number of interconnected domains grows.  As that
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   happens, the value of the identity assertion becomes equal to the
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt:   trustworthiness of the least trustworthy domain.  Since spam is a
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   consequence of the receiving domain not being able to trust the
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   sending domains to disallow the hosts in the sending to send spam,
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   the P-Asserted-Identity technique becomes ineffective at exactly the
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-   same levels of interconnectedness that introduce spam.
../data/rfc/rfc5039.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-7.2.  Misleading Results
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   Until some form of service for querying the reputation of a sending
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   agent is widely deployed, the existence of this header field
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt:   indicating a "pass" does not render the message trustworthy.  It is
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   possible for an arriving piece of spam or other undesirable mail to
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   pass checks by several of the methods enumerated above (e.g., a piece
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   of spam signed using [DKIM] by the originator of the spam, which
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   might be a spammer or a compromised system).  In particular, this
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   issue is not resolved by forged header field removal discussed above.

../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   Section 5 describes a procedure for scrubbing header fields that may
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   contain forged authentication results about a message.  A compliant
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   installation will have to include, at each MTA, a list of other MTAs
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt:   known to be compliant and trustworthy.  Failing to keep this list
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   current as internal infrastructure changes may expose an ADMD to
../data/rfc/rfc7001.txt-   attack.

../data/rfc/rfc6272.txt-   channel is clearly an important concept frequently used to mitigate a
../data/rfc/rfc6272.txt-   range of attacks.  Unfortunately, focusing only on channel security

../data/rfc/rfc6272.txt-   even some of the communication endpoints cannot be considered fully
../data/rfc/rfc6272.txt:   trustworthy, i.e., even trusted peers may act maliciously.

../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   ZRTP cryptographic key material does not create a liability for the
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   implementer from non-compliance with the ZRTP specification.
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   A user of ZRTP should note that ZRTP is not a panacea against
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   unauthorized recording.  ZRTP does not and cannot protect against an
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt:   untrustworthy partner who holds a microphone up to the speaker.  It
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   does not protect against someone else being in the room.  It does not
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   protect against analog wiretaps in the phone or in the room.  It does
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   not mean your partner has not been hacked with spyware.  It does not
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   mean that the software has no flaws.  It means that the ZRTP
../data/rfc/rfc6189.txt-   subsystem is not knowingly leaking ZRTP cryptographic key material.

../data/rfc/rfc5825.txt-   While information in any email header should usually be treated with
../data/rfc/rfc5825.txt-   some suspicion, current email systems commonly employ various
../data/rfc/rfc5825.txt:   mechanisms and protocols to make the information more trustworthy.
../data/rfc/rfc5825.txt-   For example, an organization's boundary MTA can modify "From" lines
../data/rfc/rfc5825.txt-   so that messages arriving from outside the organization are easily
../data/rfc/rfc5825.txt-   distinguishable from internal emails.  As a result of that rewriting,
../data/rfc/rfc5825.txt-   the "From" header field might not match the "Downgraded-From" header
../data/rfc/rfc5825.txt-   field.

../data/rfc/rfc5792.txt-      3986 [7].  This URI SHOULD contain instructions to update a
../data/rfc/rfc5792.txt-      particular component so that it might result in the component

../data/rfc/rfc5792.txt-      Collectors should validate that the URI and instructions come from
../data/rfc/rfc5792.txt:      a trustworthy source to avoid being tricked into performing
../data/rfc/rfc5792.txt-      damaging actions (see security considerations).

../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-   Authentication
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-      A cryptographically authenticated identity for each device is
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-      needed in an Autonomic Network.  It is not safe to assume that a
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-      large network is physically secured against interference or that
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt:      all personnel are trustworthy.  Each autonomic node MUST be
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-      capable of proving its identity and authenticating its messages.
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-      GRASP relies on a separate, external certificate-based security
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-      mechanism to support authentication, data integrity protection,
../data/rfc/rfc8990.txt-      and anti-replay protection.

../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   A DNS record that is returned as a response to a query for a Fully
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   Qualified Domain Name (FQDN) that is a subdomain of 'home.arpa.' is
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   expected to have local significance.  It is expected to be returned
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   by a server involved in name resolution for the homenet the device is
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   connected in.  However, such a response MUST NOT be considered more
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt:   trustworthy than a similar response for any other DNS query.
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   Because 'home.arpa.' is not globally scoped and cannot be secured
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   using DNSSEC based on the root domain's trust anchor, there is no way
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   to tell, using a standard DNS query, in which homenet scope an answer
../data/rfc/rfc8375.txt-   belongs.  Consequently, users may experience surprising results with

../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-   In this case the CA has issued the verifier's PSE containing the new
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-   key without updating the directory attributes. This means that the
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt:   verifier has no means to get a trustworthy version of the CA's old
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-   key and so verification fails.
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-   Note that the failure is the CA operator's fault.

../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-   with the new key without updating the directory attributes. This
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt:   means that the verifier has no means to get a trustworthy version of
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-   the CA's new key and so verification fails.
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc2510.txt-   Note that the failure is again the CA operator's fault.

../data/rfc/rfc6484.txt-   security requirements." A CP may be used by a relying party to help
../data/rfc/rfc6484.txt-   in deciding whether a certificate and the binding therein are
../data/rfc/rfc6484.txt:   sufficiently trustworthy and otherwise appropriate for a particular
../data/rfc/rfc6484.txt-   application.  This document describes the CP for the Resource Public
../data/rfc/rfc6484.txt-   Key Infrastructure (RPKI).  There are separate documents (CPSs) that
../data/rfc/rfc6484.txt-   cover the factors that determine the degree to which a relying party
../data/rfc/rfc6484.txt-   can trust the binding embodied in a certificate.  The degree to which
../data/rfc/rfc6484.txt-   such a binding can be trusted depends on several factors, e.g., the

../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-   information about gender, age, and date of birth.  SIItype values are
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-   very limited.  Therefore, it is important for users to select a
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-   fairly good password to prevent an attacker from determining whether
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-   a guessed SII is accurate.
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt:   This protocol assumes that Bob is a trustworthy relying party who
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-   will not reuse the Alice's information.  Otherwise, Bob could
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-   "impersonate" Alice if only knowledge of P and SII were used to
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-   verify a subject's claimed identity.  Thus, this protocol MUST be
../data/rfc/rfc4683.txt-   used only with the protocols that make use of digital signatures

../data/rfc/rfc4301.txt-   sources on the protected side of the IPsec boundary.  Thus, these

../data/rfc/rfc4301.txt-   counterparts arriving from sources on the unprotected side of the
../data/rfc/rfc4301.txt-   boundary.  The major security concern here is that a compromised host

../data/rfc/rfc4301.txt-   service for other devices "behind" the security gateway, or that
../data/rfc/rfc4301.txt-   could even result in violations of confidentiality.  For example, if

../data/rfc/rfc1459.txt-   restrictions on client connections (which are considered to be
../data/rfc/rfc1459.txt:   untrustworthy) than on server connections.

../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   The means to obtain the location of the revocation endpoint is out of
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   the scope of this specification.  For example, the client developer
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   may consult the server's documentation or automatic discovery may be
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   used.  As this endpoint is handling security credentials, the
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt:   endpoint location needs to be obtained from a trustworthy source.
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-Lodderstedt, et al.          Standards Track                    [Page 3]
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-
--
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   legitimate client will lose its authorization grant and will need to
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   prompt the user again.  No further damage is done and the guessed
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   token is now worthless.
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   Since the revocation endpoint is handling security credentials,
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt:   clients need to obtain its location from a trustworthy source only.
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   Otherwise, an attacker could capture valid security tokens by
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   utilizing a counterfeit revocation endpoint.  Moreover, in order to
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   detect counterfeit revocation endpoints, clients MUST authenticate
../data/rfc/rfc7009.txt-   the revocation endpoint (certificate validation, etc.).

../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-14.  Security Considerations
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   IPsec [IPSEC] can be used to address security concerns between two
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   MASC peering nodes.  However, because of the store-and-forward nature
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt:   of the UPDATE messages, it is possible that if a non-trustworthy MASC
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   node can connect to some point of the MASC topology, then this node
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   can undetectably inject malicious UPDATEs that may disturb the normal
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   operation of other MASC nodes.  To address this problem, each MASC
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt:   node should allow peering only with trustworthy nodes.
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   After a reboot, a MASC node/domain can restore its state from its
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   neighbors (internal peers, parents, siblings, children). Typically,
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   the state received from a parent or internal peer will be
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt:   trustworthy, but a node may choose to drop its own UPDATEs that were
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   received through a sibling or a child.
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   A misbehaving node may attempt a Denial of Service attack by sending
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   a large number of colliding messages that would prevent any of its
../data/rfc/rfc2909.txt-   siblings from allocating more addresses.  A single mis-behaving node
--
../data/rfc/rfc2504.txt-   is genuine.  When a 'secure' page has been downloaded, the web
../data/rfc/rfc2504.txt-   browser's 'lock' or 'key' will indicate so.  It is good to
../data/rfc/rfc2504.txt-   double-check this: View the 'certificate' associated with the web
../data/rfc/rfc2504.txt-   page you have accessed.  Each web browser has a different way to do
../data/rfc/rfc2504.txt-   this.  The certificate will list the certificate's owner and who
../data/rfc/rfc2504.txt:   issued it.  If these look trustworthy, you are probably OK.

../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt-   For large-scale groups, the IP security multicast extensions are
../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt-   dependent on an automated Group Key Management protocol to correctly
../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt:   authenticate and authorize trustworthy members in compliance to the
../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt-   group's policies.  Inherent in the concept of a cryptographic group
../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt-   is a set of one or more shared secrets entrusted to all of the Group
../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt-   Members.  Consequently, the service's security guarantees are no
../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt-   stronger than the weakest member admitted to the group by the GKM
../data/rfc/rfc5374.txt-   system.  The GKM system is responsible for responding to compromised

../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-Abstract
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   This document describes an optional method for encoding explicit
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   packet Sensitivity Labels on IPv6 packets.  It is intended for use
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   only within Multi-Level Secure (MLS) networking environments that are
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt:   both trusted and trustworthy.
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-Status of This Memo
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   This memo provides information for the Internet community.  It does
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   not specify an Internet standard of any kind.  Distribution of this
--
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   are both physically and electronically isolated from the global
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   Internet, so operators of MLS networks are not concerned about
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   external penetration (e.g., by worms, viruses, or the like).
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   Similarly, all users of the MLS network have been cleared using some
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   process specific to that organization, and hence are believe to be
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt:   trustworthy.  In a typical deployment, all computers connected to the
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   MLS network are in a physically secure room or building (e.g.,
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   protected by guards with guns).  Electronic equipment that enters
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   such a space typically does not leave.  Items such as USB memory
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   sticks are generally not permitted; in fact, often the USB ports on
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   MLS computers have been removed or otherwise made inoperable to
--
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   Label, and is usually neither imported to or exported from the
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   various Intermediate Systems it transits.  There also is the concept
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   of "CALIPSO Gateways", which import data from one DOI and export it
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   to another DOI such that the effective Sensitivity Label is NOT
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   changed, but is merely represented using a different DOI.  In other
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt:   words, such devices would be trustworthy, trusted, and authorized to
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   provide on-the-fly relabeling of packets at the boundaries between
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   complete systems of End Systems within a single DOI.  Typically, such
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   systems require specific certification(s) and accreditation(s) before
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   deployment or use.
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-7.3.4.  Security Logging
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   This option is recommended for deployment only in well-protected
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   private networks that are NOT connected to the global Internet.  By
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   definition, such private networks are also composed only of trusted
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt:   systems that are believed to be trustworthy.  So the risk of a
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   denial-of-service attack upon the logging implementation is much
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   lower in the intended deployment environment than it would have been
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-   for general Internet deployments.
../data/rfc/rfc5570.txt-

../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-8. User Agent Server Behavior
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-   Typically, a user agent renders the value of a P-Asserted-Identity
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-   header field that it receives to its user.  It may consider the
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-   identity provided by a Trust Domain to be privileged, or
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt:   intrinsically more trustworthy than the From header field of a
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-   request.  However, any specific behavior is specific to
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-   implementations or services.  This document also does not mandate any
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-   user agent handling for multiple P-Asserted-Identity header field
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-   values that happen to appear in a message (such as a SIP URI
../data/rfc/rfc3325.txt-   alongside a tel URL).

../data/rfc/rfc5755.txt-   the path from the original client, which is normally, but not always,

../data/rfc/rfc5751.txt:   message originator and therefore is only as trustworthy as the
../data/rfc/rfc5751.txt-   originator.

../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-7.2.  Misleading Results
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-   Until some form of service for querying the reputation of a sending
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-   agent is widely deployed, the existence of this header field
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt:   indicating a "pass" does not render the message trustworthy.  It is
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-   possible for an arriving piece of spam or other undesirable mail to
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-   pass checks by several of the methods enumerated above (e.g., a piece
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-   of spam signed using [DKIM] by the originator of the spam, which
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-   might be a spammer or a compromised system).  In particular, this
../data/rfc/rfc5451.txt-   issue is not resolved by forged header field removal discussed above.

../data/rfc/rfc8302.txt-
../data/rfc/rfc8302.txt-   Duplicate IP addresses within a Data Label can occur due to an
../data/rfc/rfc8302.txt-   attacker sending fake ARP/ND messages or due to human/configuration
../data/rfc/rfc8302.txt:   errors.  If complete, trustworthy directory information is available,
../data/rfc/rfc8302.txt-   then, by definition, the IP location information in the directory is
../data/rfc/rfc8302.txt-   correct.  Any appearance of an IP address in a different place
../data/rfc/rfc8302.txt-   (different edge RBridge or port) from other sources is not correct.
../data/rfc/rfc8302.txt-
